A special postgame ceremony honoring Ginobili will take place on the AT&T Center floor and will be broadcast live on FOX Sports Southwest and streamed live on FOX Sports GO. Ginobili will become the ninth player in Spurs franchise history to have his number raised to the rafters, joining Bruce Bowen (12), Tim Duncan (21), Sean Elliott (32), George Gervin (44), Avery Johnson (6), Johnny Moore (00), David Robinson (50) and James Silas (13). In 16 seasons with the Spurs, Ginobili captured four NBA championships while posting a career winning percentage of .721 (762-295), which is the best winning percentage in NBA history among players who have appeared in at least 1,000 games. S elected by San Antonio with the 57th overall pick in the 1999 NBA Draft, Ginobili appeared in 1,057 career games, averaging 13.3 points, 3.8 assists, 3.5 rebounds and 1.32 steals in 25.4 minutes. He is the Spurs all-time leader in three-pointers made (1,495) and steals (1,392), while ranking third in games played (1,057), fourth in assists (4,001), fourth in free throws made (3,380) and fifth in points (14,043). Ginobili was named to the All-NBA Third Team in both 2008 and 2011 and was a two-time All-Star (2005 and 2011). The 2008 NBA Sixth Man of the Year is the first sixth man, as well as the first South American, to earn All-NBA honors. G inobili and LeBron James are the only two players in NBA postseason history with at least 3,000 points and 300 three-pointers in the playoffs. Ginobili appeared in 218 career playoff games, averaging 14.0 points, 4.0 rebounds, 3.8 assists and 1.34 steals in 27.9 minutes. One of 10 players ever to appear in over 200 playoff games, he came off the bench 165 times and is the all-time leader in NBA postseason history as a reserve in points (2,014), assists (576), rebounds (635), steals (205), field goals made (630), three-pointers made (226) and free throws made (528). Ginobili and Tony Parker combined to record 132 playoff wins together, which are the most victories by any teammates in postseason history. After signing with the Spurs in the summer of 2002, Ginobili became one of just seven players in NBA history to spend his entire career with one team while playing at least 16 seasons, along with Kobe Bryant, Tim Duncan, John Havlicek, Reggie Miller, Dirk Nowitzki and John Stockton. He is the first South American and just the second player drafted in the second round or later to appear in at least 1,000 games with one team. Ginobili is one of only two players in history, along with Bill Bradley, to win a EuroLeague title, an NBA championship and an Olympic gold medal. A limited number of multi-game ticket plans featuring the March 28 game are available by calling 210-444-5050. Single game tickets can be purchased through Spurs.com or Ticketmaster.com. More information on Ginobili\u2019s jersey retirement will be announced at a later date."},{"id":37772,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/gregg-popovich-coaching-longevity-spurs","date":"2018-10-30","title":"Persistent Popovich, Spurs negate coaching-change ways in NBA","content":" The first coach in the Gregg Popovich era to get axed was Brian Winters on Jan. 24, 1997. He lost 100 games faster than anyone in history, a byproduct of overseeing the Vancouver Grizzlies in their expansion season (1995-96) and into 43 games of \u201996-97. The most recent to lose his job was Tyronn Lue on Oct. 28, 2018 after Cleveland\u2019s 0-6 start. This was more of a head scratcher as he\u2019s the only coach to win a title with the Cavs. Perhaps his biggest crime was failing to give LeBron James the wrong directions to Cleveland Hopkins Airport last summer. In that span, 245 NBA coaching changes were made in Popovich\u2019s time in San Antonio. Some of them have been understandable, others questionable, in all a spinning wheel that managed to eject all from the first seat on the bench \u2026 except one. In the wake of yet another coaching switch, it\u2019s fair to wonder: how and when will it end for Popovich in San Antonio? He\u2019s closer to the finish line than the starting line, but the finish line keeps moving. Any notion of Popovich vanishing once Tim Duncan, Tony Parker and Manu Ginobili left the organization died when training camp began. Any thought of Popovich turning sour from the organization\u2019s lethal relationship last year with Kawhi Leonard was dismissed when Popovich enthusiastically prepared himself for his 23rd NBA season. And all ideas of Popovich permanently drifting to one of San Antonio\u2019s relaxing 18-hole courses as he approaches his seventh decade on the planet should be shattered with a Big Bertha driver. \u201cI don\u2019t golf,\u201d he said. \u201cWhat a waste of time. I\u2019d rather read a book. You could be doing a lot of other things.\u201d Like, keep coaching. \u201cI still enjoy this,\u201d he said, before deadpanning, \u201cbut I don\u2019t know how to do anything else.\u201d He has survived this long because he wins. With 1,201 victories and counting, he\u2019s climbing toward Don Nelson\u2019s career record of 1,335 . With a straight face, Popovich says \u201cmy ass would\u2019ve been gone a long time ago\u201d if not for great success that he constantly credits to Duncan, among others. But there\u2019s another factor in play that keeps Popovich in control of his destiny and fate. He has rarely, if ever, had to answer to anyone in the Spurs\u2019 organization, now controlled by Julianna Holt, who keeps away from the basketball operation. Almost from the jump, Popovich ruled the empire, and that has separated him from others who\u2019ve won just as many, or more, than his five championships. It\u2019s a unique setup enjoyed by almost no one in professional sports, which are often controlled by owners who act on a whim. Phil Jackson (11 titles) left two organizations, including the Los Angeles Lakers twice, not totally on his own. Pat Riley had a prickly departure from the Lakers after winning four of his five career titles there. In both cases, the lines were clearly drawn: neither Jackson nor Riley, despite steering their teams to historical runs, carried the strongest voice in the building. Neither had tenure or were immune from the type of sports diseases that can fracture even dynasties and shove great coaches out the door. When he greased the \u201cShowtime\u201d era in Los Angeles, Riley had the biggest coaching profile since Red Auerbach and his signature victory cigars. Riley was charismatic, cool and changed the coaching culture. But inside was a gym rat and a clipboard scribbler. He released the leash on the fast break and made the Lakers intoxicating. He smooth-talked Kareem Abdul-Jabbar into taking a reduced role as age began to weather the Hall of Famer.  However, the core Lakers eventually grew weary of Riley\u2019s techniques and motivational tricks. When the Lakers were upset by the Phoenix Suns in the 1990 Western Conference semifinals , Riley heard the increased volume and split. Jackson\u2019s relationship with Bulls GM Jerry Krause showed decay early in the Bulls\u2019 run for a sixth and final championship in 1997-98 . Theirs was a clash of egos and ideas. That, and a demand by Jackson for more money, led to a Bulls breakup. In the early 2000s, Jackson restored the Lakers\u2019 franchise as they became the biggest rival for Popovich\u2019s Spurs in that decade. But the chore of coaxing two high-maintenance young stars, Shaquille O\u2019Neal and Kobe Bryant, beat up Jackson and lead to his first LA exit. His second stint with the Lakers ended mainly over money, among other issues. That\u2019s all foreign to Popovich, who had the benefit of taking over a team with David Robinson, the gentlemanly All-Star who gave no gruff. And then, blessed by the basketball gods, Popovich landed Duncan, the most no-frills superstar the game has ever seen. Duncan set the tone in the locker room for professionalism, conduct and work ethic. Everyone followed, something that\u2019s lasted for almost two decades, all while making Popovich\u2019s life easier (for which Popovich is forever grateful). Duncan also drastically changed the lives of two men. When Popovich stepped down from his GM role on Dec. 10, 1996 \u2013 taking the coaching job from Bob Hill after a 3-15 start \u2014 he went 17-47. That is his only losing season to date, and the Spurs fell into the Draft lottery. There were whispers at the time \u2014 blasphemy nowadays \u2014 that he might not see another season in San Antonio. In 1997, the Boston Celtics had better odds of winning Draft lottery and its grand prize: a bank-shot-shooting center from Wake Forest (via the Virgin Islands) who could transform a franchise. Had the Celtics gotten the No. 1 pick, perhaps Rick Pitino would still be coaching in the NBA instead of lobbying for a return . As much as Popovich heaps praise on Duncan, there\u2019s no denying Popovich\u2019s role in 21 straight years of playoff trips and his own coaching immortality. The way he runs an organization envied by many, helps find talent with low Draft picks (Ginobili was taken 57th overall;  Parker at No. 28 ), generates respect from players and rivals (LeBron James, among others) and is a San Antonio landmark (along with the Alamo) is no accident. If Popovich can\u2019t control his fate, then no one in his profession ever will. Besides, under what circumstances would Popovich be forced out? Even if it\u2019s his call, how will this end? He turns 70 in January, although the only time he ages is when a referee\u2019s whistle doesn\u2019t blow his way. He survived Leonard, the only documented sign of rebellion by a Spurs\u2019 star. And the Spurs, despite losing Dejounte Murray for the season to a knee injury , might keep their playoff streak alive with DeMar DeRozan blending well with new teammates. \u201cIt\u2019s San Antonio, OK? The faces have changed but the standards are the same and the way do things are the same,\u201d Popovich said. \u201cWe\u2019re going to expect the guys to do their jobs on and off the court. None of that\u2019s going to change. The way we want to approach the game and have the respect for the game is all the same, just with different people.\u201d Asked about the Murray injury and other non-Spurs-like issues, he adds: \u201cMaybe we deserve a little bad luck. We got to draft Tim Duncan 20 years ago. So, a little misfortune. We deserve it.\u201d Coaching changes since Dec. 1996 Gregg Popovich was named coach of the San Antonio Spurs on Dec. 10, 1996 . Since then, there have been 245 coaching changes league-wide. Here\u2019s a look at how many changes each team has gone through in the Popovich era. In two years, Popovich assumes control of the US Olympic basketball team . That could satisfy his urge to coach without the 82-game grind and free up time to pursue other stuff. But who knows? \u201cBeing a wine consultant going from vineyard to vineyard, or a restaurant critic going from restaurant to restaurant, that would be more fun, for sure,\u201d Popovich said. The 1996-97 season was bloody for the profession. Seven teams, including the Spurs, changed coaches in season. The Washington Bullets (now Wizards) had three coaches that season. And, in fact, Bernie Bickerstaff held two jobs that season, resigning as Denver\u2019s coach in November and was later hired by Washington in February. Cotton Fitzsimmons lasted eight games with the Phoenix Suns. Only one new coach that season lasted more than two decades. Since Popovich\u2019s debut, the Utah Jazz have had the fewest coaching changes (two), while the Grizzlies and Wizards have been on the other extreme (13 each). The Dallas Mavericks\u2019 Rick Carlisle and the Miami Heat\u2019s Erik Spoelstra own the longest tenure after Popovich (10 years each). We\u2019ll never see another like him in our lifetime. He\u2019s a coach who gets results on the court, respect in the locker room and no orders from above. Good luck finding another combination like that. The 245 coaching changes are not a number Popovich particularly likes (because he sticks up for the profession) and it\u2019s not a number that he\u2019ll add to anytime soon \u2014 if he has any say. Which he does. \u201cI\u2019m a simple untalented man,\u201d he said. \u201cThis is all I can do. I\u2019d better stick with it.\u201d * * * Shaun Powell has covered the NBA for more than 25 years. We missed you. We wondered where you were. Surely not behind the 5-for-36 3-point stat line through the season\u2019s first seven games. That couldn\u2019t have been you. It seemed even more impossible after you single-handedly decimated the city of Chicago on Monday night . The Windy City was pockmarked with your NBA record 14 3-pointers \u2014 a feat that surpassed some dude named Steph. By the time your work was done and Chicago was ashes, you\u2019d registered 52 points in just over 26 minutes. We tried to find out who else had tossed up 50-plus in under 27 minutes. Basketball Reference kindly informed us that there were \u201c no results that match your search .\u201d The Warriors found you, though. And kept finding you. When you were sitting on 13 triples, one away from breaking your own teammate\u2019s record, they kept feeding you. They wanted you to break the record, especially after all the \u201cWhere\u2019s Klay?\u201d inquiries tied to your unlikely cold spell. When you finally did hit that last 3, it was only after you got your forehead gashed open and wore a headband over your war wound. You made Michael Jordan\u2019s former franchise want to go hide in a hole. When you pour boiling water into a cold glass, it cracks. On Monday, you cracked the NBA universe. Again . Welcome back, man. Thompson\u2019s outburst was directly tied to Golden State\u2019s mammoth offensive output against the Bulls. The Warriors led 92-50 at halftime , good for the second-highest first-half total in NBA history, behind only Phoenix\u2019s 107 against Denver in 1990. Golden State wound up making a franchise-record 24 3-pointers, also second-most all-time. Klay\u2019s big night should also have a drastic effect on his season shot chart.   Speaking of long-awaited returns, give it up for Karl-Anthony Towns. Minnesota\u2019s recently maxed big man, who may or may not want a certain teammate out of town , tossed up 25 points and 16 rebounds in Minnesota\u2019s 124-120 win over LeBron and the Lakers. It was Towns\u2019 best game since netting 31 points against Dallas on Oct. 20 \u2014 a game in which Jimmy Butler did not play. This time, both Towns and Butler (32 points) dominated on the same night, giving Minnesota a much-needed win during an uncertain time.  L.A.\u2019s loss only emphasized an early trend on the defensive end. After Monday night, LeBron\u2019s Lakers are solidly in the league\u2019s bottom 10 in defense. Their biggest chink in the armor: The paint, where they give up the third-most points in the league (56.6 ppg) behind only Phoenix and Houston. The clash of the last two unbeaten teams wasn\u2019t exactly star-studded. Giannis Antetokounmpo (concussion) and Kawhi Leonard (rest) both sat as their respective teams matched their unblemished records in Milwaukee. The Bucks ultimately prevailed 124-109 behind double-digit scoring from seven different players. Ersan Ilyasova (19 points, 10 rebounds) was especially effective filling in for Antetokounmpo, and early Coach of the Year candidate Mike Budenholzer will surely remember that if the Bucks ever decide to play small with the Greek Freak at center. For now, Milwaukee stands alone atop the league thanks to its best start (7-0) since Lew Alcindor changed his name to Kareem Abdul-Jabbar (1971).  Lance Stephenson and Gorgui Dieng are buddies, a relationship that includes having a laugh at the other\u2019s expense. Stephenson did just that when Dieng playfully shoved him after an offensive foul call. The enigmatic Laker immediately seized the opportunity to cry wolf by dramatically staggering and drawing the nearby official\u2019s attention. Dieng was assessed a technical foul, an unjust punishment in which Stephenson openly reveled. It\u2019s not just the Warriors that are on fire. The entire league is playing a caffeinated version of basketball not witnessed since the run-and-gun 1980s. In October 2017, teams scored at least 110 points on 80 different occasions. Through Monday night, teams had surpassed 110 points a whopping 114 times. That\u2019s a 42.5-percent increase, and we\u2019ve still got two days left in the month! Sacramento sporting a 3-3 record was fun, but admittedly deceiving. The Kings\u2019 three wins came at the expense of the Thunder, Grizzlies and Wizards, teams that boast a combined record of 5-11. But the Heat are good! They\u2019re a playoff team boasting continuity, a quality coach and decent offensive and defensive numbers through the first two weeks of the season. The Kings took those facts and turned them both upside-down, earning a quality 123-113 win on the road. Buddy Hield, Willie Cauley-Stein and De\u2019Aaron Fox \u2014 three of Sacramento\u2019s most important young players \u2014 each scored 20-plus. Again, it\u2019s early. The Kings were 4-3 as recently as 2014, before an injury to DeMarcus Cousins and a coaching change sent them plummeting to their usual lottery depths. Champions and MVPs are crowned when leaves are blooming, not when they\u2019re falling. And the notion that anything in October means much in the Eastern Conference has been a quaint one for, oh, about eight years now. But LeBron James vacated the East, there\u2019s an air of openness to the conference now and the oh-so-early clash between the NBA\u2019s two remaining unbeaten teams Monday night carries a bit of buzz. It\u2019s the Toronto Raptors vs. the Milwaukee Bucks in the latter\u2019s new Fiserv Forum tonight ( 8 ET, NBA League Pass ). As much as folks in Boston or Philadelphia might want you to believe otherwise, this isn\u2019t merely a JV game. It\u2019s early, sure, but it\u2019s a clash that could get dusted off and referred to prominently when the East finals begin in May. Only one of the two will wake up Tuesday with an unblemished record, reason enough to pay attention. Seven games \u2014 they\u2019re both 6-0 now \u2014 out of 82 is equivalent to a 2.25 mile marker in a marathon. And neither has spent much time punching in its proper weight class; the Raptors\u2019 and Bucks\u2019 opponents sport a combined 27-47 record to this point. Still, a dozen of those defeats have come against the East aspirants who will meet for the first of four times, with a rematch in Toronto Dec. 9 and two more in January. Toronto, of course, has reached these heights before. It has finished first (four times) or second (once) in the Atlantic Division for five straight seasons and was No. 1 overall in the East last season. The Raptors have a new coach, but it\u2019s a fellow \u2014 Nick Nurse \u2014 who was on the staff last season when they revamped their offense. They swapped out one all-NBA player, DeMar DeRozan, for another in Kawhi Leonard. (Leonard will miss tonight\u2019s game for rest reasons .) Milwaukee? Not so much. The Bucks cracked .500 the past two seasons but are 60 games under .500 over the last five seasons. Last season was a wasted year instead of the big, bold step forward the franchise craved. It is the team with a new coach and staff, Mike Budenholzer bringing an unabashed commitment to the 3-point game from Atlanta. It has several new faces to aid in that transformation, including Ersan Ilyasova, Brook Lopez and rookie Donte DiVincenzo. Returnees such as Khris Middleton and even John Henson are shooting with the arc in mind, too. Then there\u2019s Giannis Antetokounmpo, whose fast start and burgeoning game should render the flashy new arena as one of Milwaukee\u2019s subplots, not needed for any honeymoon effect. (Unfortunately, he also will miss this game as he was placed in the NBA\u2019s concussion protocol Monday.) \u201cNew arena, new practice facility, new coach,\u201d Antetokounmpo said after an opening-week victory over Indiana. \u201cThis is the right time to be a Buck.\u201d It\u2019s a pretty swell time to be a \u201cGreek Freak\u201d freak, too. Antetokounmpo\u2019s energized start \u2014 he\u2019s averaging 25 points, 14.2 rebounds and 5.7 assists per game \u2014 is most notable because he\u2019s doing it in 30.7 minutes per game. The Bucks needed only a strong initial push against the fractured Timberwolves in Minneapolis Friday, then had to settle Saturday for just 19:23 of Giannis as he dealt with the reverberations of an Aaron Gordon elbow in the first quarter against Orlando. Milwaukee won the games by 30 and 22 points, respectively. Per 36 minutes, Antetokounmpo\u2019s numbers are career bests \u2014 29.3 ppg, 16.6 rpg, 6.7 apg and two bpg \u2014 and his usage rate (36.7 percent) tops the league. (James Harden\u2019s was 36.1 when he won Kia MVP honors last season.) Antetokounmpo leads the league with 30 turnovers and he is 1-for-16 himself on 3-point attempts. But he\u2019s not backing off on the latter, having worked on his long-distance shooting all summer, and he likely will clean up the former as he masters the spots where Milwaukee\u2019s shooters like to launch their own. If Antetokounmpo with reliable 3-point accuracy is the rest of the NBA\u2019s greatest nightmare, having him surrounded with four other deep threats is a close second. The team\u2019s defensive improvement under Budenholzer has been profound \u2014 from a 109.1 rating last year ( 18th in NBA ) to today\u2019s 97.8 (2nd in NBA). But the stretching of Milwaukee\u2019s offense has been gaudier. Last season, the Bucks took 29.7 percent of their shots from behind the arc. So far this season? They\u2019ve launched 43.6 percent from back there , second only to Houston\u2019s 44.6 percent. They\u2019re on pace to shoot 3,266 3-pointers, a year-over-year increase of 61.3 percent. They let fly with 47 of them against the Pacers and outscored their guests by 33 points on 3-pointers. They\u2019ve made 90 so far to the other guys\u2019 63. And the greatest beneficiary of the opened offense has been Antetokounmpo, the man who invades the paint either to attack at the rim himself or to wheel and deal, kicking out to one of multiple options. \u201cOne man can\u2019t stop him all by himself,\u201d said Indiana\u2019s Victor Oladipo. \u201cWe need to load to him. \u2026 Once he finds his rhythm he starts finding guys.\u201d Said Pacers reserve Doug McDermott: \u201cIt\u2019s a five-man job. \u2026 We weren\u2019t plugging the gaps and that\u2019s what you have to do against him. You have to pick your poison a bit though, because they have a lot of shooters now.\u201d Milwaukee has weapons, Toronto has experience and new looks, neither of them has a defeat and folks in the East have some new alphas to watch. If you have an issue with the rankings, or have a question or comment for John Schuhmann, send him an e-mail or contact him via Twitter . * * *"},{"id":37816,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/kemba-walker-qa-hornets-season-scoring","date":"2018-10-29","title":"Q&A: Hornets' Walker starts season in scoring groove","content":" With the new season underway, and with his game as hot as almost anyone to start, Charlotte Hornets guard Kemba Walker was asked what impressed or surprised him about the first 10 days or so of 2018-19. \u201cNothing besides my own play,\u201d Walker said, laughing after a shootaround Friday. \u201cNothing besides seeing my name near the top of the NBA scoring, which is pretty weird.\u201d Eh, maybe not so weird. Walker, a two-time All-Star, is the Hornets\u2019 all-time leading scorer . At 28, the former ninth overall pick in the 2011 Draft is in his prime as a player. The 41 points he dropped on Milwaukee on opening night and the fact he\u2019s gone for at least 23 every game since (with three more games of 30 or more) seems like the next logical step. It earned him the season\u2019s first Eastern Conference Player of the Week honor and as Week 2 ended, his 31.7 ppg trailed only Golden State\u2019s Stephen Curry (33.9) and Portland\u2019s Damian Lillard (33.8).  \u201cIt was [gratifying]. Who wouldn\u2019t want it to keep going?\u201d Walker told NBA.com. \u201cI know teams will be gearing up on me and double-teaming me. But I just want to win, man. I want to get back to the playoffs any way possible. I don\u2019t care what I average the rest of the year.\u201d Walker, in the final year of a four-year, $48 million deal he signed in 2014, never has shot the ball so well \u2014 40.5 percent from the arc, 46.6 percent overall. Neither has he shot it so often and from such range. Walker is averaging 23 shots, including more than 11 3-point attempts. His usage rate of 33.5 trails only Milwaukee\u2019s Giannis Antetokounmpo (35.1) and his 29.4 PER puts him ahead of Kevin Durant and LeBron James. Is it sustainable? That was one of multiple topics Walker talked about with NBA.com\u2019s Steve Aschburner:  *** Steve Aschburner:  On Media Day, you made it sound as if you would hit this season hard from the start, and that\u2019s exactly what we\u2019ve seen. How do you explain it? Kemba Walker:  I knew I had a good summer. I put in the work and the time and the effort to get better. And I\u2019m healthy \u2014 I haven\u2019t felt healthy like this in a long time. Over the last three summers, I wasn\u2019t healthy, having knee surgeries and \u2018scopes. So I was rehabbing. This summer, I had a chance to work on my game. Being able to work on my shooting over a long period of time really helped as well. SA:  You took as many 3FGAs last season as you shot your first two seasons combined. Now you\u2019re launching them at a pace (11.3 per game) to break Steph Curry\u2019s single season record (886). Is this a conscious change by you or a reaction to the league\u2019s preferred style? KW : Both. The league definitely has changed from the time I first came in. Everybody\u2019s shooting more threes, no matter their position. Me, I\u2019ve just become more confident. I worked on my shot tremendously to get to this point. I\u2019m comfortable now shooting it, whenever I can get to my spots. SA:  What\u2019s your preference \u2014 pull-up threes, spot-up threes or those halfcourt threes like Steph takes? KW:  Not at all [laughing]. Steph is a different type of shooter, maybe the best to ever shoot the basketball. But I\u2019m comfortable shooting them however. It doesn\u2019t matter. If I can get \u2018em up, I try to make \u2018em. But I do love for my teammates to create for me and get me some easy ones. It does take some stress, some pressure, off of me. SA:  Your coach, James Borrego, has talked of using you more off the ball. Does that suit you? KW:  It really helps. It gets me a little bit of rest, and it opens up a different dynamic in my game. As well as giving other guys a chance to have the ball in their hands and create for others. But the main thing is, it just keeps me fresher, which is huge for me. SA:  What\u2019s your take on the Charlotte rookies? KW:  Oh, I\u2019m a huge fan. Devonte\u2019 [Graham] really hasn\u2019t gotten a chance to play yet, but I\u2019ve always been a huge fan, even when he was at Kansas. Just love his game, love his poise. And that\u2019s skill \u2014 I don\u2019t think people understand how much of a skill it is to be poised, especially at a young age. It\u2019s something that I didn\u2019t have, something that took me a very long time to get. Miles [Bridges], he\u2019s a hard-playing kid. Smart, always in the right spot on both ends of the floor. I can see him getting more minutes as the season progresses.  SA:  Malik Monk is a second-year guy who didn\u2019t have the most satisfying rookie season. What do you see from him, and can he become a reliable backcourt mate? KW:  Oh yeah, he\u2019s growing. Every single day. His efficiency will come. He needs time to learn, needs time to develop, to figure out where his shots are going to come. He\u2019s getting better already. He\u2019s passing the ball really well, getting other guys involved. He needs to know we need him every night, with him coming off the bench for us. SA:  Your rookie season was about as challenging as could be \u2014 delayed by a lockout, rushed through training camp and a quickie preseason, and then a 7-59 experience. Did that set you back as a player? KW:  Nah, it wasn\u2019t a setback. It was humbling. I took it as a point in my career where I was going through adversity. It was tough \u2014 nobody likes to lose \u2014 and through my basketball career I felt I had been a winner. But I just stuck to it, just kept working hard. SA:  You said you don\u2019t want to talk anymore about your free agency next summer \u2014 and your general manager, Mitch Kupchak, is on record saying, \u201cOur intention is for him to end his career in a Hornet uniform.\u201d Some people wonder what the market might be, though, given how many terrific point guards are out there. So let\u2019s address that another way: what is it like competing with all those rivals? KW:  It\u2019s unbelievable, man. Every night. Every single night, somebody is there to \u2026 I can\u2019t even explain it. Every team, there\u2019s so many great point guards out there who are just ready to showcase their talents. There are young guys ready to show how good they are. Yeah, it\u2019s a point guard league. SA:  We\u2019re seeing more and more teams switching everything defensively. How hard is that on a 6-foot-1 point guard? KW:  It\u2019s \u2026 tough sometimes. Some matchups, you don\u2019t want to get. But I rely on my teammates to help out as much as possible. The most challenging part probably is boxing guys out. But I\u2019m always up for the challenge. SA:  Some players talk or at least play like defense is optional. Your thoughts? KW: Not at all. I\u2019m paid to do it all. It\u2019s not even about being paid \u2014 I\u2019m just competitive. I want to play defense. I want to score. I want to do it all. SA:  I\u2019ve often wondered what it\u2019s like to play for the team that Michael Jordan owns. Other teams, the owners aren\u2019t basketball experts. But that\u2019s not the case for the Hornets. Is it intimidating? KW:  I wouldn\u2019t say intimidating. I love it. I want my owner to have played. He knows what\u2019s going on, he knows how it feels after losses, after wins. Traveling. Being tired. He\u2019s been through it. He knows what it takes to win games in this league. Even though basketball\u2019s a bit different now from when he played, but still, he knows. I feel like I\u2019m at an advantage because I can go to him, I can ask him things. Or he can just come to me, or text me or call me to let me know things. And let me know how to get past things. No, it\u2019s an honor for us, it\u2019s an honor for me to have him as an owner. SA:  How is basketball different from when Jordan played? KW:  For me, just the threes. A lot of bigs shooting threes. The bigs are different in general, you know? Back with MJ, I feel like the shooting guards and the forwards were dominant, and it was more of a post-up league. Now it\u2019s a point guard\u2019s league for the most part. And it\u2019s not a post-up league much anymore. There are so many threes up in the air. SA:  Do you little guys resent the stretch-fours and stretch-fives coming out onto your turf these days? KW:  Yeah, man, it\u2019s crazy. But it\u2019s fun. Just seeing the development and the change. Even from when I first got in the league it wasn\u2019t like that. But guys are so talented nowadays, it\u2019s unbelievable. SA:  Tell me about the Big Brothers Big Sisters work you do, mentoring four kids \u2014 two boys and two girls \u2014 in the Charlotte area. KW:  Just to be in their lives. I take \u2018em out to eat, take \u2018em to Dave & Buster\u2019s every now and then. It\u2019s fun. I try to avoid the cameras. It\u2019s not for social media. It\u2019s not for anything but them. The kids are doing great in school. That\u2019s the biggest progress, that\u2019s what you want. They\u2019ve really started to love basketball now \u2014 they come to games sometimes. It\u2019s been fun to see them grow, each and every time I see them. One of the kids, his mom passed away. I know it\u2019s been a struggle for him. For me to be able to help get his mind off of that for a time, just be there for him, that\u2019s definitely rewarding for me but I hope it\u2019s more rewarding for him. SA:  You\u2019re in your eighth season, and you\u2019ve played a total of 11 playoff games. What stands out for you about the postseason? KW:  I remember every game. We played Miami twice. The first year [2014] was when they had LeBron, Dwayne Wade and Chris Bosh. They swept us, but I thought we played really well. Obviously it wasn\u2019t enough \u2014 they had three Hall of Famers. I remember the level of intensity those guys played with. I remember telling myself, the next time I get to the playoffs, I\u2019m going to try my best to play like that. The next time [2016], that\u2019s what I did. People thought we might get swept again, but we went to seven games. It was really fun. The whole atmosphere was so intense. I loved it. You have to take your game to a whole \u2018nother level. You have to play hard every possession, every second of those games. The competitiveness, the toughness, everything goes up. SA:  A problem that team had, it still has \u2014 you\u2019re carrying such a big load offensively. Do you need a second reliable scorer, and is that guy on the roster now? KW:  Of course. We need it. I\u2019m not going to have huge games every night. It\u2019s on one of these guys to step up. I think guys are still searching for their roles at this point, especially with a new coach, new system. We\u2019re still learning. But as the season progresses, I think they will. We have guys who are capable of putting points up for us. SA:  The All-Star Game this season is in Charlotte. You\u2019ve been selected twice. What would you think of playing in that game in your market? KW:  That\u2019d be amazing. To be in Charlotte, the team that drafted me, the team I\u2019ve played with for eight years now, it would be a really special moment. Hopefully I can get there. It\u2019d be fun. A really important and fun moment in my career. * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. You can e-mail him here , find his archive here and follow him on Twitter . Expectations were sky-high for Olajuwon after he entered the league as the No. 1 Draft pick out of the University of Houston. Olajuwon not only met those expectations during his career, he surpassed them to quickly become one of the best players in the NBA. Olajuwon joined forces with reigning Rookie of the Year Ralph Sampson in Houston to form a dynamic front-court that helped the Rockets reach the playoffs for the first time since 1981. During his rookie season, Olajuwon averaged 20.6 points, 11.9 rebounds and 2.7 blocks in 35.5 minutes over 82 games. He was named the 1985 Rookie of the Year, earned his first of 12 All-Star selections, and selected to the NBA All-Defensive Second Team. Olajuwon, which translates into \u201calways being on top,\u201d was one of the best players in the NBA during his remarkable 18-year career. He retired in 2002 with career averages of 21.8 points, 11.1 rebounds, 3.1 blocks and 1.7 steals. He was a two-time NBA champion with Houston, two-time NBA Finals MVP, 1994 NBA MVP, 12-time All-Star, two-time Defensive Player of the Year, and six-time All-NBA First Team member. There\u2019s also a third layer to this unfamiliar skin, and this could be the most uncomfortable of them all. Already the West shows few signs of fall-off from a year ago when 10 teams finished with winning records. The reflection doesn\u2019t pain him anymore. From the outside, the Cleveland Cavaliers\u2019 All-Star forward appears to have it all: A model\u2019s striking looks, a multi-million dollar contract and dream job. At 30, he\u2019s in the prime of his career, and maybe for the first time, truly happy. \u201cI\u2019m getting there,\u201d he said, his voice conveying determination. \u201cIt\u2019s still a work in progress.\u201d Nearly a year ago, Love suffered a panic attack during a game against Atlanta. The desperate, life-altering event eventually led to revealing his long-term battle with anxiety and depression. Now Love is hoping to break down stigmas about men\u2019s mental health. He has partnered with Shick Hydro on a website series called \u201cLocker Room Talk\u201d , holding candid conversations with Olympic gold-medal swimming icon Michael Phelps, former Boston Celtics star Paul Pierce and Cavs teammate and close friend Channing Frye. No topic is off limits. The first episode with Phelps premieres next month and coincides with fundraising efforts for the Movember Foundation, which promotes men growing moustaches during the month to raise awareness for prostate cancer, testicular cancer and suicide. Love recently filmed the sit-down talks with Phelps, Pierce and Frye on Cleveland\u2019s east side while his Cavs prepared to open their first season in five years without superstar LeBron James, who signed with Los Angeles this summer. While Love knows his 11th year as a pro will be challenging ( Cleveland fell to 0-4 on Wednesday night ), his focus was on the series, which he views as an important step in his personal development and a natural progression toward a broader platform for more mental health initiatives.  Love\u2019s discussions with the three athletes cover a wide range of topics, including \u201cmasculinity, traumatic events, women and their roles in our lives, thoughts of suicide, anxiety. Everything.\u201d \u201cWe\u2019re changing the paradigm of locker room talk where it\u2019s not just about sports anymore,\u201d he said. \u201cIt\u2019s life.\u201d In their talks with Love, Phelps and Pierce open up about their past struggles with mental health. Frye brings a perspective of coping with loss and tragedy following the deaths of his parents within weeks of each other in 2016. Love hopes the frank discussions will touch people to seek help and maybe encourage them to address their problems. It took a distressing experience for Love to take the first steps in his journey. During the second half of a game on Nov. 5 against Atlanta, Love\u2019s career took an unexpected turn and it may have saved him. He suffered a panic attack so severe that Love said he felt like he was dying. He felt trapped, gasped for air and with his heart and mind racing uncontrollably, Love frantically scrambled from room to room deep inside Quicken Loans Arena before finally collapsing on the locker room floor. He was taken to the Cleveland Clinic for tests. Love already had a sense of what was wrong. He had struggled since childhood with depression and mood swings, which he said was passed down through generations in his family. As a teenager, he would often descend into \u201cmy dark place\u201d for weeks and experienced fits of rage. \u201cI was angry and would break stuff,\u201d Love said. \u201cI would never hurt anyone, but I would act out against authority and rebel. I didn\u2019t know why I felt that way.\u201d He subsequently sought counseling. I still don\u2019t know how I came out and played 48 hours later. I wanted to hide. I was so afraid that people were going to find out what had happened. All those events that then followed allowed me to get to a point where I was like, \u2018OK, now it\u2019s time for me to tell my story\u2019 and I wanted to tell it in my own words.\u201d Love remains overwhelmed at the outpouring since revealing his struggles. "},{"id":37867,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/nba-scoring-across-league","date":"2018-10-24","title":"Scoring in NBA currently surpassing historic levels","content":"  (AP) \u2014 NBA scoring is up. Way up. At this pace, that won\u2019t change anytime soon. Through the first eight full days of this season \u2013 obviously, a small sample size \u2013 NBA teams are averaging 113.4 points per game, the highest rate in 49 years. Teams are averaging 91 field-goal attempts per game, up five shots from last season, and the league is on an early pace to set records for 3-pointers made and attempted for the seventh straight year. \u201cI think guys are just getting more comfortable offensively in their games,\u201d Toronto point guard Kyle Lowry said. \u201cI think everyone is just expanding, with the centers shooting 3s. I think that just opens the floor up a lot more. A lot more shots are going up, a lot more freedom of movement. It makes it more of an exciting game.\u201d As recently as the 2014-15 season, teams were reaching the 100-point mark 51.5 percent of the time. So far this season, that figure is at 88.2 percent. And there are no shortage of other statistically baffling numbers out there in the early part of this season. In the 51 games played through Tuesday, 49 have seen at least one team score 100 points. The losing team has wound up scoring 100 or more points a staggering 80.4 percent of the time. Minnesota scored 136 points in a non-overtime game and lost \u2013 the first time that\u2019s happened in the NBA since 1992. New Orleans posted a league-high 149 points against Sacramento last week. San Antonio scored 143 , albeit in an overtime game, against the Los Angeles Lakers, the most by the Spurs in more than eight years. (The Spurs also allowed 142 points in the same game, the most points allowed by a San Antonio team since 1990, long before Gregg Popovich\u2019s coaching tenure there started.) \u201cThe whole dynamic of the game has changed,\u201d said Spurs guard DeMar DeRozan, in remarks published by the San Antonio Express-News . \u201cThe rules force you to play at a high speed. Tempo is getting pushed. So many 3s are being shot.\u201d Perhaps the most baffling stat is this: Entering Wednesday, LeBron James and the Lakers ranked No. 2 in the NBA in scoring at 125.3 points per game, and were off to an 0-3 start anyway. \u201cWe\u2019ve played well enough to win \u2026 and well enough to lose,\u201d James said. \u201cIf that makes any sense.\u201d In this wide-open NBA, that might make perfect sense. The league\u2019s officials are emphasizing what the NBA calls \u201cfreedom of movement\u201d this season, an effort to clean up some of the extra contact that happens away from the ball and impedes offensive players from cutting and getting open. And it\u2019s no surprise that the tighter whistles are an advantage to the offense, though they are hardly the only reason why scoring is soaring. NBA teams were called for 19.9 fouls per game last season; this season, it\u2019s up to 23.1. Free throws taken and made are also up slightly, though nowhere near enough to account for the entire spike in scoring. It\u2019s the sum of many parts \u2013 faster pace, perhaps caused in part by that extra freedom of movement, along with more 3s being taken. And again, it\u2019s early. Players will likely fatigue over the course of the season, and at some point defenses will adjust and catch up somewhat. \u201cI\u2019m sure some of this will level off,\u201d Miami coach Erik Spoelstra said. \u201cHow much it\u2019ll level off, I don\u2019t know. It\u2019s just about developing a system, a way of playing, an identity defensively that\u2019s important to you.\u201d"},{"id":37864,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/luke-walton-fined-postgame-comments-spurs","date":"2018-10-24","title":"NBA fines Lakers coach Luke Walton $15,000","content":"Los Angeles Lakers head coach Luke Walton has been fined $15,000 for public criticism of the officiating, it was announced today by Kiki VanDeWeghe, Executive Vice President, Basketball Operations. Not to worry. There will be plenty of options other than basketball for fans after a nearly $200 million arena renovation. Want to work on your swing? Check out the golf suites. Need a trim? Yep, there\u2019s a barber shop. \u201cThis arena is for every single fan in the city of Atlanta,\u201d said Brett Stefansson, the general manager who oversaw the project. \u201cWe wanted to touch every aspect of it and make it special and unique.\u201d The Hawks will debut their newly renamed State Farm Arena on Wednesday night, hosting the Mavericks in a home opener that features two of the NBA\u2019s most touted rookies, Atlanta\u2019s Trae Young and Dallas\u2019 Luka Doncic. But both teams are in the midst of massive rebuilding projects that will likely take years to pay off \u2013 they posted matching 24-58 records a year ago and could be even worse this season \u2013 so fans might be checking out some of the arena\u2019s new amenities. There are all sorts of fancy food items, from chicken and beignets to lemon pepper wings to a signature Italian pizza. Grammy-winning country star Zac Brown has his own place, which includes Southern-style gourmet dishes crafted by his personal chef and music memorabilia. There are also three ritzy courtside clubs \u2013 including one which has a bar area shaped like the Hawks logo behind a basket, where fans can sip cocktails while they watch the game. More budget-minded patrons can take advantage of a dozen discounted items such as $1 chips, $2 jumbo pretzels or a $4 bottomless bucket of popcorn, seizing on a popular trend that started next door at Mercedes-Benz Stadium, home of the NFL Falcons and Atlanta United soccer team. But it\u2019s some of the other features that figure to draw much of the attention in the new-look arena. Most notably, there is Killer Mike\u2019s Swag Shop, a partnership with the Atlanta-based rapper that includes a full-service barber shop with four chairs facing the court on the second level, allowing customers to keep up with the home team while getting a fresh cut. Or, if the game gets out of hand, the Topgolf Swing Suites might be a welcome alternative. While fans won\u2019t be able strike actual golf balls onto the court, they can try their luck on the simulators. Hawks guard Kent Bazemore, an avid golfer, plans to slip up there himself before a game. \u201cI\u2019ll be up there smacking it around soon,\u201d he said, breaking into a sly grin. Stefansson said the designers were focused on \u201ccreating a lot of different and distinct destinations, things than fans want to go seek out and experience.\u201d Bazemore called it a sign of the times, given that fans have myriad entertainment options and ever-shrinking attention spans. \u201cIt\u2019s all about the experience,\u201d he said. The aesthetics didn\u2019t go overlooked, either. Massive clear glass windows were installed, providing views of the surrounding downtown area. Numerous walls were knocked down, allowing those on the concourses to keep up with the game even when they venture from their seats. \u201cPeople actually communicating, meeting new people, standing on the concourse talking about the game, it just opens it up for that community feel that we\u2019re trying to push here in Atlanta,\u201d Bazemore said. The project also drew its share of criticism over $142.5 million in public funding , which cleared the way for the second-biggest project of its type in NBA history. It was surpassed only by Madison Square Garden in New York, where a $1 billion upgrade was carried out over three offseason from 2011-13. This renovation \u2013 or \u201ctransformation,\u201d the preferred nomenclature of its supporters \u2013 was largely carried out over the past two summer breaks, a frenzied job that allowed little room for dallying or delay. The most striking part of the project was the removal of the stacked suites on one side of the building formerly known as Philips Arena , which required the Hawks to play all of last season with a giant covering over that construction zone and reduced capacity to just over 16,000. Even now, with much of the work complete, the arena will seat only about 16,500 for basketball, a drop of almost 3,000 seats from the original configuration when the arena opened in 1999. But the Hawks have traditionally been one of the NBA\u2019s worst-drawing seats, so there wasn\u2019t much hesitation about cutting back capacity to allow for a major upgrade. \u201cThe first thing we knew needed to do was knock down that wall of suites. Take that premium area and redistribute it around the building a little more evenly,\u201d Stefansson said. \u201cIt creates a lot more balance in the building.\u201d Many of the Atlanta players got their first look at their revamped home on Tuesday. After playing their first three games on the road, the Hawks practiced for nearly two hours on the newly installed court, beneath a massive new scoreboard and video screen. They were impressed with the new surroundings, which includes a much larger locker room and training area. Who\u2019s the league\u2019s tallest player? Which team has the oldest average age? What jersey numbers are the most popular? All of that data and more was compiled in the 2018-19 NBA Roster Survey . Here are nine takeaways \u2026 > Complete 2018-19 NBA Roster Survey (PDF) * * * 1. Each season, the league uses its findings to determine who is the \u201caverage\u201d NBA player, with Thunder small forward Andre Roberson fitting the bill this season. The average player in the league is 26 years old, 6-foot-7, 219.05 pounds and has 4.8 seasons worth of NBA experience. Roberson, 26, is listed at 6-foot-7, 210 pounds and has played five seasons in the league. He awaits the start to his sixth season as he rehabs from patellar tendon surgery. 2. There are still two active players in the league who were born in the 1970s: Vince Carter and Dirk Nowitzki. Meanwhile, the youngest player in the league hadn\u2019t been born when Michael Jordan won his sixth and final championship in 1998. Isaac Bonga of the Lakers was born on Nov. 8, 1999, meaning next year there will be an NBA active player born in 2000. 3. Another wild takeaway in the age category is that out of 440 players in the league, 418 of them are younger than 33-year-old LeBron James, who in his 16th season is still playing at an MVP level. 4. The Cavaliers were the oldest team in the NBA last season, but that changed following James\u2019 defection to the Los Angeles Lakers. The Rockets now hold that title (28.6 years old), with the New York Knicks officially labeled the youngest team in the league (24.57).  5. The Nuggets have a roster that uniquely ranks second to last among teams with the most NBA experience (an average of 3.27 years). The Bulls have the lowest average in the league (3.13), followed by the Nuggets, Knicks (3.47), Celtics (3.53) and Magic (3.53). 6. The Knicks\u2019 Kristaps Porzingis and Clippers\u2019 Boban Marjanovic are once again the tallest players in the league at 7-foot-3, with 5-foot-9 Nuggets point guard Isaiah Thomas being the league\u2019s shortest player. A few names that fit the average NBA height of 6-foot-7 inches tall: DeMar DeRozan, Luka Doncic, Draymond Green, Kawhi Leonard and Klay Thompson. Additionally, the Nets, Nuggets, Grizzlies and Kings all sport rosters with an average height of exactly 6-foot-7. 7. Despite having the NBA\u2019s rebounding leader in 6-foot-11 Andre Drummond, the Detroit Pistons check in as the shortest team in the league overall (6 feet, 6.27 inches). The Washington Wizards are the NBA\u2019s tallest squad on average at 6 feet, 8.15 inches. 8. The University of Kentucky remains the top college represented on NBA rosters, with a total of 27 players hailing from the collegiate powerhouse. Duke (23), UCLA (15), Kansas (12) and North Carolina (11) round out the top five. 9. There are 20 players who wear No. 3, the most popular jersey number worn in the NBA. Guards Chris Paul, Dwyane Wade, Bradley Beal, CJ. McCollum each don the digit Allen Iverson once wore. This partnership continues Las Vegas\u2019 steadfast commitment to the sport of basketball, which also includes our annual support of four collegiate conference basketball tournaments, NBA Summer League and USA Basketball Men\u2019s National Team training camp and exhibition games.\u201d For the first time, the NBA G League Winter Showcase will host more than 20 players from NBA Academies, the league\u2019s network of elite basketball training centers for top prospects from outside the U.S. The prospects will scrimmage against one another, participate in the 2018 Tarkanian Classic and receive life-skills programming focusing on education, leadership, health and wellness, and character development. The MGM Resorts NBA G League Winter Showcase has become a springboard to the NBA from its official minor league, with more than 60 players earning GATORADE Call-Ups during or immediately following the last 14 Showcases.  Last season, 11 players signed NBA contracts coming out of the Showcase, including Westchester Knicks guard Trey Burke. He went on to average 12.8 points and 4.7 assists while shooting 50.3 percent from the field in 36 games with the New York Knicks. The full game schedule is below. (AP) \u2014 A planned museum will tell the story of basketball great Larry Bird and his Indiana roots. Indiana Gov. Eric Holcomb announced Saturday that the museum will be part of a convention center being built in Terre Haute in western Indiana. Bird plans to donate personal items and memorabilia from his career with the Boston Celtics, Indiana State University, the U.S. Olympic team and beyond, The Tribune-Star reported. Holcomb predicts the museum will be a global draw, describing Bird as \u201cLarry the Legend \u2013 Indiana\u2019s favorite son.\u201d \u201cTo have this museum, which symbolizes what being a Hoosier is all about \u2013 hard work, determination and striving to be the best \u2013 and to have that in Terre Haute, that\u2019s just a match made in heaven and I can\u2019t wait to see it,\u201d Holcomb said. Bird grew up in French Lick, Indiana. He created excitement during his days at Indiana State University in Terre Haute when he led the school to the NCAA title game in 1979, although the Sycamores lost to Magic Johnson\u2019s Michigan State team. Bird\u2019s school pride carried over to his NBA career as a player, coach and executive. When the Celtics won the 1984 NBA Championship, Bird dedicated the win to Terre Haute. Details about the museum are still being developed, but plans include interactive displays about Bird\u2019s life and career. He won three NBA championships with the Celtics. Construction on the convention center is expected to start in the spring. Ten different teams have scored at least 125 points in a night , and there hasn\u2019t been a single overtime game this season. Pace has been a factor. Through 39 games, the league has averaged 103.4 possessions (per team) per 48 minutes, up from 98.1 last season. That\u2019s a huge jump, but not as huge if you look at where we were at this point last year. Through six days (40 games) last season, the league was averaging 101.3 possessions (per team) per 48 minutes, and that mark dropped as the year went on. But efficiency has also been a part of the high scoring. The league has averaged 109.5 points scored per 100 possessions, up from 105 at this point last season. Effective field goal percentage is up, free throw rate is up, and turnovers are down. And though the pace may slow down in the coming weeks and months, efficiency typically goes up as the season goes on. So we may be in for a historically good offensive season. In regard to offense, the New Orleans Pelicans are leading the way , and are one of six teams that are undefeated entering Week 2. LeBron James is two games into his latest adventure \u2014 you may have heard about it \u2014 with the Los Angeles Lakers. Chris Bosh, the third member of the Heat \u2019 s pioneering \u201c Heatles \u201d trio of the early 2010s, has not played in the NBA since February of 2016 due to a blood clotting condition that the NBA has ruled a career-ending illness. But he has a new team of his own as well. Bosh is diving into esports arena, taking on the role of Player Management Advisor with Gen.G , cultivating a player development program for the global esports and entertainment outfit. Bosh said the talents and skills necessary to be successful in this endeavor are closely aligned with the talents and skills he sharpened over the years from his time as a high school All-American in Dallas to his standout season at Georgia Tech and, finally, to his championship NBA and international career. An 11-time NBA All-Star and two-time champion with the Heat, Bosh, 34, reflects on his new role with Gen.G, how a life competing at the highest level in basketball prepared him for what comes next, the impact the groundbreaking moves made by the Heat\u2019s \u201c Big 3 \u201d had on the NBA landscape and beyond with NBA.com \u2019s Sekou Smith. * * * Sekou Smith:  Former Lakers standout Rick Fox was one of the first former NBA players to go all-in on the gaming world. What is it about esports that speaks to the interest and talents of guys such as yourself? Chris Bosh:  I think the team concept. People are going to be competing on a worldwide basis. And you are competing to be the best at something. That definitely speaks to guys in the NBA because it\u2019s such a small fraternity and we understand how hard it is to get there and how hard it is to maintain. I mean, Rick Fox being a champion and an actor at the same time, he had a different understanding of things. He understands teamwork on a championship level. He understands it on a really bad level, when you are a part of teams that aren\u2019t great, and then again he understands it on a great level because he\u2019s been to there. It\u2019s the same for me. We understand things from top to bottom. We can lend a level of expertise in this young industry. And it\u2019s important. It\u2019s truly going to be a worldwide sport. And it\u2019s only going to get bigger the more connected the world is and the faster the internet gets. And I think it\u2019s going to be a phenomenon that only continues to grow and we\u2019re going to look back at it in five or 10 years and be like, \u2018man, I remember when nobody even knew about esports. And now everybody is going to aspire to be a part of that.\u2019 SS:  Is that something that, at this stage of your life, you look back and realize is not normal \u2026 the fact that you\u2019ve been training to compete on the biggest stage since you were probably in middle school? CB:  Absolutely. I do kind of marvel at my journey. But at the same time, I look back at a lot of the steps, and maybe not every step, but a lot of the steps, and since I was 12 or 13, I look at the people who helped me and the moments that were inspirational to me and you can recall what my mindset and what my make-up was at that time to try and be the best. And making that decision with myself to really go for it, in whatever I was doing. So, it\u2019s one of those things where I\u2019ve always been very passionate about teamwork and team building. And yeah, I remember how it\u2019s made me feel and how I\u2019ve triumphed over different situations and it helped me get to this point now. And that\u2019s one of the main things that I try to implore young people to \u2014 video games, basketball, football, lawyer, doctor \u2014 anything you want to do or be, you\u2019ve got to make a decision or a choice to be the best. And you\u2019ve got to work at it. You\u2019ve got to go for it. And you know what? You\u2019re going to fail and you\u2019re going to come up short at times, and you have to do it for a very long time, you have to be committed to calling yourself the best. You have to be ready to compete. Because lots of people dream that dream, to be the best at something. But are you ready to commit to the hard work that it takes to get there? For me, it just became a part of my life and it was what it was. Another day, another basketball practice or another game. And it\u2019s just more work. It\u2019s always about the work. SS:  When you\u2019ve competed on the stage that you have, from being a state champion and McDonald\u2019s All-American and college star and top five NBA Draft pick, is it really difficult at this stage of your life to find anything else that pushes that same button \u2026 the competitive fire button that gets the adrenaline flowing and satisfies that hunger for elite-level competitiveness? CB:  Well, you know what, I\u2019ve just kind of left it where it is. Unless I\u2019m competing at something, I don\u2019t worry about it, trying to fill the void or match that feeling I had. But it is tough. And it is very hard. That\u2019s why I make it a point to try not to pay attention to it or to try and fill the void. Because you can\u2019t. It\u2019s impossible and it won\u2019t happen. The NBA is the best league in the world. It\u2019s no way you\u2019re going to be able to fill the void that the energy of performing before 20,000 people every night gives you. That\u2019s just impossible. So, I just try and move on from that and let it be a moment in time and leave it alone. I don\u2019t look for that to fulfill me and I\u2019m moving on to different things. Now that\u2019s \u2026 if I do play again, I\u2019m not going to say hey, \u2018I\u2019m this player that I was.\u2019 I\u2019m going to be a totally different player in a totally different situation and bring all those experiences with me. But looking to fill voids, it\u2019s one of those bottomless pits, man. It\u2019s not a ghost I\u2019m interested in chasing.  SS:  Clearly, you still look at the league from your unique perspective and still pay close attention to the game. Are you surprised at all at just how radically the game has changed schematically since you, LeBron James, Dwyane Wade, Carmelo Anthony and that celebrated draft class of 2003 first hit the scene? CB:  Yeah, I\u2019m surprised but I\u2019m not surprised. I am surprised at the speed and the 3-point shooting, of course. I\u2019m with the 3-point shooting, as you know. Not all [threes] are good [threes]. But I\u2019m more surprised by the pace of the games than I am anything. That\u2019s the main thing I\u2019m looking at now. I\u2019m like, \u2018whoa, these dudes are flying.\u2019 The game has gotten a lot faster. I was anticipating changes as far back as when I first game into the league, when the spacing was changing and big guys were playing in different spaces on the floor. But when that change actually happens, and it happens so quickly, it\u2019s just amazing. Everyone has to have the skills now, everybody is switching everything now, everybody is going to the Warriors now [laughing] \u2026 it\u2019s just different. SS:  You, LeBron and D-Wade took so much grief for teaming up in Miami. And now it\u2019s like everybody wants to pair up and make sure they go somewhere with other superstars and chase the dream. CB:  Yeah, everybody is getting smarter. SS:  Do you think, in terms of eras and the way the game is viewed, you are the guys who ushered in a new era in terms of players recognizing their true power and exercising that in free agency or whatever way they can? CB:  I think so, yeah. I mean, we definitely thought outside the box a bit. And that\u2019s what kind of changed the whole landscape, because it was moreso a situation where, \u2018hey, I\u2019m going to lock into this and that\u2019s going to be it because that\u2019s how you do it.\u2019 And it doesn\u2019t necessarily have to be that way. You can make your own decisions. So that\u2019s really the event or thing that started this whole narrative. Indeed, in savaging the Cavaliers for 35 points and 11 assists , Young achieved a stat line not seen from an NBA rookie since Curry himself in 2010, and only 11 rookies overall. Per Basketball Reference , that list of 11 rookies includes such legends as Michael Jordan, LeBron James, Oscar Robertson and Jerry West. Add Young\u2019s six 3-pointers, and the list shrinks to just Curry and Jason Kidd. Add Young\u2019s single turnover, and the list shrinks to just \u2026 Young. It was a welcome showing for the Hawks, who have been treated to flashes from Young since NBA Summer League but nothing like Sunday\u2019s outburst, in which he sank 13 of 23 shots overall and orchestrated the offense like a seasoned veteran. And it came in just his third NBA game. Given his dearth of experience, and the setbacks that no doubt lay ahead for a rebuilding Hawks team pegged to again finish near the bottom of the standings, it would be foolish to anoint Young this early. After all, he only just turned 20 last month. But for at least one historic afternoon, the Curry comparisons seemed pretty dead-on. (If nothing else, Doncic was impressed! ) Most players might ease themselves back into action in their first game back after needing a month to recuperate from knee surgery. Most players are not Russell Westbrook. It\u2019s obviously still early, but the NBA\u2019s per-game scoring average entered Sunday at a whopping 113.1 points, its highest since 1969-70. ( Seven , to be exact.) Again, this was unusual; Ingram is calm by nature and has never earned a flagrant foul in his two-plus seasons. And then, every October, the young guys begin their real rookie transition. Consider Wendell Carter Jr. of the Chicago Bulls. In a span of five days, he will have gone through a gauntlet of imposing NBA big men that would have some 10-year veterans flinching and wondering if their tendinitis needed a night off. Carter\u2019s on-the-job rigors began Thursday, when he became only the 10th Bulls rookie to start on opening night and was met in his matchup at center with Philadelphia\u2019s Joel Embiid. It continued Saturday in Chicago\u2019s home opener against Detroit, with Carter banging at various times against both Andre Drummond and Blake Griffin. Now the 19-year-old will travel to Dallas , where he\u2019ll get his first test against the Mavericks\u2019 salty DeAndre Jordan. And just for the record, in the Bulls\u2019 final preseason game , he had to cope with Denver\u2019s crafty Nikola Jokic. For someone so young, against such a slate of established or eventual All-Stars, Carter\u2019s early lessons have been difficult. There really is no other way. \u201cI\u2019m sure it\u2019s just chaos and confusion right now for him,\u201d Griffin said after leading the Pistons with 33 points, 12 rebounds and five assists in a 118-116 victory at United Center. \u201cHe doesn\u2019t look that way, but that\u2019s just how you feel \u2013 no matter what \u2013 when you\u2019re a rookie. The game is moving so fast.\u201d Carter, the No. 7 pick from Duke in this year\u2019s Draft , fell victim to foul trouble early and the Bulls\u2019 need to play catch-up late, which had coach Fred Hoiberg sticking with Jabari Parker at the end. Carter logged less than 18 minutes, finishing with eight points, two rebounds and two blocks. Drummond had foul issues of his own, exiting with his sixth after just 23:33. Still, Drummond and Griffin won the frontcourt battle with 43 points and 25 boards to Carter and Bobby Portis\u2019 combined 14 and 16. It wasn\u2019t the sort of Windy City debut Carter would have scripted. This was, after all, kind of a big deal \u2013 he\u2019s the player Chicago landed after an entire 2017-18 season spent gaming the NBA\u2019s Draft lottery system. The Bulls consciously tried to dive deep, won a little too counterproductively in December and January and wound up waiting until after the first six picks were gone. That tortuous process led everyone to Saturday, when 21,289 in the stands got their first official look at the alleged silver lining from last season\u2019s dark cloud. Carter wasn\u2019t happy with either his or his team\u2019s performance afterward, pulling his clothes from the hangers in his locker as he dressed and bemoaning the Bulls\u2019 lack of defensive communication (they\u2019ve given up 245 points in two games). Not to worry, though, Griffin said. \u201cHe\u2019s so talented, he\u2019s going to be fine,\u201d the Pistons star said. \u201cIt\u2019s just a matter of time for him. I watched him play probably more than any other player in college last year \u2013 I really like his game. I\u2019ve known of him since he was in high school. He would be the least of my concerns if I was over there in the front office or on the coaching staff.\u201d Hoiberg and his staff have approached Carter\u2019s trial by fire by starting him in response to the challenges he handled in summer league and in the preseason. He arrived with a maturity, poise and defensive bent some players never achieve \u2013 a young Al Horford was a frequent comp \u2013 and isn\u2019t about to blow that image, no matter how many lumps he takes. \u201cI appreciate the fact I\u2019m able to play against these very talented bigs early on in my career,\u201d Carter said. \u201cLearn what I\u2019ve got to work on. I\u2019ve got to get stronger, that\u2019s the first thing I recognized. \u2026 Just being up against the best, I love the competition. I love going against the best players.\u201d Truth be told, Hoiberg said he talked with Carter on Friday about handling the frustrations he\u2019ll surely encounter. He\u2019s a little cranky about the officiating, for example, picking up at least three fouls in all six preseason and regular-season appearances while playing fewer than 23 minutes every time. He\u2019s does the \u201cverticality\u201d thing as if from a textbook and still hears a whistle. \u201cAt this point, I just feel like it\u2019s rookie calls. I don\u2019t care what nobody\u2019s saying, that\u2019s how I really feel,\u201d Carter said. \u201cI still have respect for the game, though. I have respect for the referees. If they call it, it\u2019s a foul. I\u2019ve just got to do better, learn from it.\u201d Then there was the chatter from Embiid in Philadelphia, a 19-point Sixers romp. \u201cHe was telling me what I should and shouldn\u2019t do,\u201d Carter said. \u201c\u2018C\u2019mon rookie, you\u2019ve got to do\u2019 something \u2018better.\u2019 Carter didn\u2019t chatter back, he said. \u201cNot yet. I\u2019m gonna get there at some point though.\u201d Drummond didn\u2019t pile on, thanks perhaps only to the referees. \u201cIf I played more, I think it woulda been more of a schooling,\u201d the Detroit center said. \u201cThis is a helluva three games for him.\u201d Drummond, 25, remembers what it was like six years ago, when he was the one absorbing the lessons. His rookie year got dinged 22 games due to a stress fracture in his back, an injury that compounded the basketball education. \u201cI learned my lessons the hard way,\u201d Drummond told NBA.com. \u201cPhysically. I started out being hurt. I had to just play and figure it out game by game. Watched films. Learned the guys that I played against. And figured it out.\u201d Drummond wound up averaging 7.9 points, 7.8 rebounds and 1.6 blocks. He had nine double-doubles and earned all-rookie status. But he\u2019s glad to be wiser now in the NBA\u2019s ways, given how few the shortcuts were. \u201cIt was more of a sponge season for me,\u201d he said. \u201cLearning the NBA. I mean, I was a young kid. Just tried to have fun with it. It was the game I loved and I was playing it at the highest level, so I just tried to enjoy every moment and take it in.\u201d That\u2019s Carter today, way at the front end of his career. He\u2019s got a notebook, he said, that he scribbles in bullet points, tips and lessons from each game after he\u2019s left the arena, his mind clear. Portis said he\u2019ll share more with Carter as the season goes on \u2013 there hasn\u2019t been much time and the Bulls haven\u2019t really hit the road yet \u2013 but most of this stuff will be hands-on. \u201cIt\u2019s as important a thing as you\u2019re going to face in this league,\u201d Hoiberg said. \u201cWhen you\u2019ve got a 19-year-old kid out there, it\u2019s human nature I think when you\u2019re playing against an opponent like Wendell has gone against, to hang your head a little bit.\u201d The coach added: \u201cIt\u2019s something every player goes through in this league. It\u2019s understanding who you\u2019re playing against. We\u2019re showing him a lot of personnel, film on who he\u2019s going to be going up against.\u201d Until the day, and it will come, when young guys are studying film of Carter, going through gauntlets of their own. * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980.
